Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection against the definition of species and its rebuttal. The objection claims that the genus (e.g., animal) is said of many differing in number, just like the species, so the definition of species as 'a universal said of many differing in number' is not exclusive. The objector gives examples: 'animal' is the answer to both 'What is Zayd and 'Amr?' (two humans) and 'What is this horse and that horse?' (two horses). Therefore, how can the definition of species exclude genus? The author answers that this objection only applies if the definition of species includes the phrase 'agreeing in essence' (muttahidūn bi al-ḥaqīqa). But here the definition says 'without the essence' (dūna al-ḥaqīqa), i.e., differing in number but not in essence. So the objection is avoided. To explain: 'animal' correctly answers a question only if the question includes individuals differing in essence (like humans and horses). Even if the question also includes individuals agreeing in essence (like two humans), the answer still covers the differing essences. So the genus applies to many differing in number when the question involves multiple essences, whereas the species applies to many differing in number but agreeing in essence. Thus, the definitions remain distinct. The passage adds that the objection is also denied because the answer 'animal' is given only because the question includes two different essences (human and horse); the individuals agreeing in essence (e.g., two humans) are treated as a single type. So the answer is not simply about differing in number but about differing in essence. Therefore, the definition of species as said of many differing in number without essence stands.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): In this passage, genus (jins) refers to a universal that answers 'What is it?' for multiple kinds differing in essence, e.g., 'animal' for humans and horses. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
النوع (al-naw'): Species defined as 'a universal said of many differing in number but not in essence' (dūna al-ḥaqīqa). [Note: Standard definition; no significant shift.]
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqa): Essence or quiddity; the real nature shared by individuals of a species. [Note: Stable in logic, though in theology it may have additional connotations.]
مختلفين (mukhtalifīn): Differing; used in two ways: differing in number (bi al-‘adad) and differing in essence (bi al-ḥaqīqa). [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objection that 'animal' is said of many differing in number not refute the definition of species as said of many differing in number without essence?
A) Because the definition of species includes 'agreeing in essence'.
B) Because 'animal' as a genus is only said of things that agree in essence.
C) Because the answer 'animal' is valid only when the question includes individuals differing in essence, not merely differing in number.
D) Because the definition of species is not concerned with number.

Key Takeaway
The definition of species remains exclusive because genus requires difference in essence, while species only requires difference in number within a shared essence.

The Differentia: Distinguishing What Something Is in Its Essence
Author defines the differentia as answering 'which thing in its essence' and distinguishes essential, accidental, and absolute types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differentia (fasl) in a definition and distinguish between essential, accidental, and absolute differentiators.

Core Idea
The differentia answers 'which thing?' not 'what is it?' — it is the essential mark that separates species within a genus.

In logic, defining a thing requires not only placing it in a genus (its 'what'), but also distinguishing it from other members of that genus. This distinguishing feature is the differentia (fasl). The passage clarifies how to identify the differentia by the type of question it answers, and how to classify differentiators as essential, accidental, or absolute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question the differentia answers
The differentia is said in answer to 'which thing is it in its essence?' (أي شيء هو في ذاته), not 'what is it?'. This contrasts with the genus.
The genus answers 'what is it?' (ما هو).
2
Distinguish essential vs accidental differentiator
If the differentiator is restricted to essence (ذاتي), it is essential (e.g., rational for man). If restricted to accident (عرضي), it is accidental (e.g., laughing). If unrestricted, it is absolute.
Accidental differentia is not a proper part of strict definition.
3
Apply to an example
For 'man' (الإنسان), its genus is 'animal'. The differentia is 'rational' (الناطق) because it distinguishes man from other animals.
This example is from the passage itself.
4
Note the connection to genus
Every quiddity (ماهية) that has a differentia has a genus. The author points out this reminder from al-Shifa'.
Later logicians preferred a different account from al-Isharat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِمَّا غَيْر مقُوْلٍ في جواب ما هوَ، بَلْ مقول في جوابِ أَيُّ شيءٍ هو في ذَاتِهِ). فإِنَّ السؤال بأي شيء هو إِنَّما عَنْ المُميّز. فإن قيّد بقوله: في ذاته، فعَنِ المميّز الذاتي). وإِنْ قُيّد بقوله : في عَرَضِهِ ، فعن المميّزِ العَرَضيّ وإذا أُطْلِقَ فَعَنِ المميّز المُطْلَقِ؛ ولذا قال: (وهو الذي يُميّزُ الشَّيءَ عمَّا يشارِكُهُ في الجِنْسِ كالناطِقِ) بالنسْبَةِ للإنسان) تنبيها على أنَّ كلَّ ماهية لهَا فَضْل فلها جنس البتَّةَ وهو المذكور في الشفاء). وأمَّا المتأخرون فاختاروا المذكور في الإشارات:
And it is not said in answer to 'what is it?', but is said in answer to 'which thing is it in its essence?'. For the question 'by which thing is it?' is only about the differentiator. If it is restricted by saying 'in its essence', then it is about the essential differentiator. If it is restricted by saying 'in its accident', then it is about the accidental differentiator. And if it is left unrestricted, then it is about the absolute differentiator. Therefore he said: 'It is that which distinguishes a thing from what shares with it in the genus, like rational with respect to man', as a reminder that every quiddity that has a space has a genus absolutely, and it is mentioned in al-Shifa'. As for the later ones, they chose what is mentioned in al-Isharat.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the differentia (fasl). It begins by distinguishing the differentia from the genus: while the genus is said in answer to 'what is it?' (e.g., 'animal' for man), the differentia is said in answer to 'which thing is it in its essence?' (e.g., 'rational'). The differentia is thus a differentiator (mumayyiz). The author then classifies the differentia based on whether the question is restricted or absolute. If restricted to essence, it is an essential differentiator (mumayyiz dhati), like 'rational' for man. If restricted to accident, it is an accidental differentiator (mumayyiz 'aradi), e.g., 'laughing' for man. If left unrestricted, it is an absolute differentiator (mumayyiz mutlaq), which can be either essential or accidental. The author then quotes Ibn Sina's al-Shifa', illustrating the differentia as what distinguishes a thing from what shares its genus. This reminder implies that every quiddity with a specific difference has a genus. Later logicians, such as those following al-Isharat, preferred a different account, but the core definition remains.

Key Terms
الفصل
المميّز
المميّز الذاتي
المميّز العرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'What is it?'
B) 'Which thing is it in its essence?'
C) 'What is its genus?'
D) 'How many species?'

Key Takeaway
The differentia is the distinguishing mark that separates a species within its genus, answering 'which thing in its essence'.

The Specific Difference and Its Types
Author defines the specific difference and its near and far types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between near and far specific differences and apply the definition of fasl to exclude other universals.

Core Idea
The specific difference answers 'what kind' and is part of the essence.

In this passage from the Fawaid Fanari, the author defines the specific difference (fasl) as a universal that distinguishes a species from others. He distinguishes between the near fasl (e.g., rational for humans) and the far fasl (e.g., sensitive). The definition also sets boundaries against genus, species, general accident, and property. By understanding these distinctions, you will sharpen your ability to analyze definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined: the specific difference (فصل). The passage gives both near and far types, then a general definition.
2
Distinguish near and far specific difference
Note the examples: near fasl = 'rational' (ناطق) under the near genus 'animal'; far fasl = 'sensitive' (حساس) under the far genus 'body'. Understand the difference in terms of genus proximity.
3
Extract the definition (rasm)
The definition: a universal predicated in answer to 'what kind of thing is it?' (أي شيء). Analyze each part: 'universal' excludes particulars, 'predicated of a thing' indicates it is a predicate, 'in answer to what kind' distinguishes it from genus and species.
4
Test exclusion of other universals
Check why genus, species, general accident, and property are excluded based on the definition's conditions. Genus and species answer 'what is it?', not 'what kind'; general accident is not predicated in any essence question; property is not part of the essence (hence 'in its essence' excludes it).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواباً عن الماهية وجميع المشاركات في ذلك الجنس. كالناطق والحيوان. ٢. والبَعيدُ: إن ميزَهُ عن المشارِكَاتِ في الجنس البعيد، الذي لا يَصِحُ جَوَاباً عن الماهِيَّةِ وجميع مشاركاتها في ذَلِكَ الجِنسِ كالحسّاس والنامي. ويُرْسَمُ بأَنَّهُ كلّيّ، يُقالُ على الشيء في جَوابِ: أيُّ شيءٍ هو؟ يَخْرُجُ به الجِنْسُ والنَّوعُ لعدم مقوليتهما في جَوَابِ أَيِّ شيءٍ، بل في جواب ما هُوَ؟ والعرَضَ العام، لعدم مَقُوليَّته في الجَوابِ أصلاً، وبقوله: (في ذَاتِهِ) يَخْرُجُ بِهِ الخاصة.
As an answer about the quiddity and all the participants in that genus, like 'rational' and 'animal.' 2. And the far: that it distinguishes it from the participants in the far genus, which is not valid as an answer about the quiddity and all its participants in that genus, like 'sensitive' and 'growing.' And it is defined as a universal predicated of a thing in answer to 'what kind of thing is it?' Thereby genus and species are excluded for not being predicated in answer to 'what kind of thing?' but rather in answer to 'what is it?' And the general accident is excluded for not being predicated in the answer at all. And by his statement 'in its essence,' the property is excluded.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage from the Fawaid Fanari defines the specific difference (فصل) and its two types. The text first describes the near specific difference (الفصل القريب) which distinguishes the species from others sharing the immediate genus, giving the example of 'rational' for human among animals. Then it describes the far specific difference (الفصل البعيد) which distinguishes from those sharing a remote genus, like 'sensitive' and 'growing' for human among substances. The passage then provides the definition (rasm) of the specific difference: it is a universal that is said in answer to 'what kind of thing is it?' (أي شيء هو). This definition excludes genus and species because they answer 'what is it?' (ما هو), not 'what kind.' It also excludes general accident because it does not answer any question about essence. The phrase 'in its essence' (في ذاته) excludes property (خاصة) because property is not part of the essence. This is a typical logical definition in the Ilm al-Mantiq tradition, setting precise boundaries for the concept of specific difference.

The Nature of Definition: Must Be Compound
Author introduces second chapter on concepts and argues definition must be compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a definition must be a compound statement according to the passage.

Core Idea
A definition is a compound statement because only compound expressions can convey essence.

This lesson begins the second chapter of logic focusing on concepts (tasawwurat). The author defines the explanatory statement (al-qawl al-sharih) and argues that a definition must be a compound of at least two words. Understanding this foundational point is essential for building correct definitions in all Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Find 'al-qawl al-sharih' and 'al-muarrif' in the Arabic. Note that they are used synonymously here. The first term means 'explanatory statement' and the second means 'definition'.
2
Understand the reason for 'statement'
The author says a definition is called a 'statement' (qawl) because a statement is compound, and a definition is compound. A qawl in logic is a compound expression of two or more words.
3
Recognize the disagreement
There are two views among logicians: (1) all definitions are compound (the view of early logicians), and (2) most definitions are compound but a single word can sometimes serve (the view of later logicians).
The author sides with the first view.
4
Identify the author's position
The author states 'the correct is the first' meaning all definitions are compound. The passage then begins to give a reason, but it is cut off.
5
Apply the principle
Recognize that a definition must be a compound that expresses the essence. This is foundational because any definition in Islamic sciences (e.g., of 'salah' or 'zakat') must be articulated as a compound to avoid ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني مقاصد التصورات) وهو باب القول الشارح) ويُرادِفُهُ المعرّف). ويُسمى قَوْلاً؛ لأنَّ القَوْلَ هوَ المركَّب، والمعرِّفُ مُرَكبٌ كُليّا عند قوم، وغالباً عند آخرين). والصحيحُ هوَ الأَوَّل)، لا لأَنَّ المُعرِّفَ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ النظَر:
The second chapter: Purposes of Concepts. It is the chapter of the explanatory statement, and it is synonymous with 'definition' (al-muʿarrif). It is called a 'statement' because a statement is the compound, and the definition is compound, according to some people completely, and according to others generally. The correct is the first, not because the definition is from the types of investigation (nazar)...
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage introduces the second chapter of logic concerned with concepts (tasawwurat). It defines the explanatory statement (al-qawl al-sharih) as synonymous with definition (al-muʿarrif). The author explains that a definition is called a 'statement' (qawl) because a statement is composed of at least two words, and a definition is typically compound, such as 'rational animal'. There is disagreement: according to early logicians (al-mutaqaddimun), all definitions must be compound; according to later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirun), most definitions are compound but a single word can serve as a definition in some cases. The author states that the correct view is the first (all definitions are compound), and then begins to offer a proof, which is cut off in this extract. The discussion touches on the fact that definition is a kind of investigation (nazar) which involves arranging known things to reach an unknown. In the Islamic tradition, this is crucial because definitions form the foundation of syllogistic reasoning, and a proper definition must be articulated as a compound expression that conveys the essence.

Circularity in Defining 'Nazar' and the Inclusion of Simple Terms
Author explains the circularity in the definition of nazar and the need to include 'tahsil amr'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circularity in a definition and explain how adding a disjunctive element can resolve it.

Core Idea
Defining nazar only as 'arrangement' leads to circularity; adding 'acquisition' breaks the circle.

The definition of 'nazar' (inquiry) as 'an arrangement of known things' seems straightforward, but the author reveals a hidden assumption: it assumes definition by a simple term is invalid. This assumption leads to a vicious circle. To resolve it, later logicians redefined nazar disjunctively, allowing both simple and composite definitions. In this lesson, you will trace the circularity and see how a subtle adjustment fixes it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original definition of nazar
The passage begins with the definition: nazar is 'an arrangement of known things' (tarteeb umoor ma'loomah). Note that this definition implies a composite structure.
This definition is common in early logic texts.
2
Recognize the hidden assumption
The author points out that this definition is based on the premise that definition by a simple term (ta'rif bil-mufrad) is invalid. Why? Because arrangement only applies to composite things, so if nazar is arrangement, it cannot be simple. That assumption is carried implicitly.
Definition by a simple term would be something like defining 'man' as 'rational'.
3
Trace the circular dependency
The passage spells out the circle: (1) Inv alidity of definition by simple term is based on every nazar being composite. (2) Every nazar being composition is based on the definition of nazar as arrangement. (3) But that definition itself is based on the invalidity of definition by simple term. Thus, the invalidity of definition by simple term is used to prove itself—a circle.
In syllogistic terms, the conclusion becomes identical to one premise, violating the rule that the conclusion must differ from the premises.
4
Understand the logical flaw
The circularity arises because the definition of nazar already presupposes that definition by simple term is invalid. This makes the argument unproductive. In a valid syllogism, the conclusion cannot be one of the premises.
The author explicitly states that the conclusion became identical to the minor premise.
5
See the resolution
To break the circle, some scholars redefined nazar as 'the acquisition of a matter or an arrangement of things' (tahsil amr aw tarteeb umoor). By adding 'acquisition of a matter', they allowed definition by a simple term (e.g., 'acquiring the concept of rationality') to count as nazar, thus removing the dependency on the invalidity of simple definitions.
This disjunctive definition includes both simple and composite inquiries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي هو تَرْتِيبُ أُمورٍ مَعْلَوْمَةٍ؛ فإِنَّ كَوْنَ النَّظَرِ تَرْتِيْبَ أُمُورٍ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى عَدَمِ صِحَّةِ التَعْرِيفِ بالمُفْرَدِ). فلو كان ذلك مبنيّاً على هذا لَزِمَ الدَّوْرُ ؛ ولِهَذا عَرَّف بَعْضُهُم النَظَرَ : بِتَحْصيلِ أَمْرٍ أَوْ تَرتيبٍ أُمُورٍ". (۱) أي: لا يكون المفرد معرّفا؛ لأنَّ المفرد لا يُطْلَقُ عليه ترتيب أمورٍ معلومة؛ لأنَّ الترتيب هذا لا يكون إلا مع المركب. (۲) الدور يكون هكذا: عدم صحة التعريف بالمفرد مبني على كون كل نظر مركبا. وكون كل نظر ترتب أمور معلومة. مبني على كون كون كل نظر ترتيب أمور معلومة. وكون كل نظر ترتيب أمور معلومة مبني على عدم صحة التعريف بالمفرد. فيكون عدم صحة التعريف بالمفرد مبنيا على عدم صحة التعريف بالمفرد. أي: يجب أن تكون النتيجة غير إحدى المقدمتين، وهنا صارت النتيجة هي نفس الصغرى، ومن شروط الإنتاج أن لا تكون النتيجة عين إحدى المقدمتين. (۳) لأجل القول بجواز التعريف بالمفرد، مع أنه نظري، اضطر أن يعرف النظري: بأنه تحصيل أمر أو ترتيب أمور. فقد اضطر إلى زيادة كلمة (تحصيل أمر ليجعل من النظر تحصيل أمرٍ أو ترتيب أمور؛ ليشمل الأول التعريف بالمفرد.
Which is an arrangement of known things; for the fact that consideration (nazar) is an arrangement of things is based on the invalidity of definition by a simple term. If that were based on this, circularity would follow. Hence some defined consideration (nazar) as: the acquisition of a matter or an arrangement of things. (1) That is: the simple term is not a definer, because the simple term is not described as an arrangement of known things, for arrangement only occurs with a composite. (2) The circularity is as follows: The invalidity of definition by a simple term is based on every consideration being composite. And every consideration being an arrangement of known things is based on the fact that every consideration is an arrangement of known things. And every consideration being an arrangement of known things is based on the invalidity of definition by a simple term. So the invalidity of definition by a simple term is based on the invalidity of definition by a simple term. That is: the conclusion must be different from one of the premises, and here the conclusion became identical to the minor, and among the conditions of productivity is that the conclusion not be identical to either premise. (3) For the sake of allowing definition by a simple term, despite it being theoretical, one was compelled to define the theoretical as: the acquisition of a matter or an arrangement of things. He was compelled to add the word (acquisition of a matter) to make consideration (nazar) into the acquisition of a matter or an arrangement of things, in order to include the first, which is definition by a simple term.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage critically refines the definition of 'nazar' (consideration/inquiry) to avoid circularity. The author begins by stating the existing definition: nazar is an arrangement of known things. He then points out a problem: this definition implicitly assumes that definition by a simple term (mufrad) is invalid, because arrangement only applies to composites. But if that assumption is the basis for the definition, it leads to circularity. The circularity is spelled out: the invalidity of definition by a simple term depends on every nazar being composite, and every nazar being an arrangement of known things depends on the same thing, and that in turn depends on the invalidity of definition by a simple term—so it is a circle. In logical terms, the conclusion (invalidity) becomes identical to one premise, violating the rule that a valid syllogism's conclusion must differ from its premises. To remedy this, some scholars redefined nazar as 'the acquisition of a matter or an arrangement of things' (tahsil amr aw tarteeb umoor). By adding 'acquisition of a matter', they allowed definition by a simple term (like defining 'man' as 'rational') to be considered a legitimate nazar, thereby breaking the circularity. The passage thus shows a careful logical critique of a definition and the constructive response to fix it.

نظر
دور
تعريف بالمفرد

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must avoid circularity; adding disjunctive options can resolve hidden circular dependencies.

Why Every Definition Must Be Composite: Hadd vs Rasm
Author argues every definition must be composite, then distinguishes hadd and rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a definition must be composite and distinguish between hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition).

Core Idea
A definition is always composite because it requires predicating one concept of another, forming either an essential or descriptive definition.

In this passage, the author explains a fundamental principle of definition logic: no single term can stand alone as a definition. Every definition must be composite, involving a predicative relation. Moreover, the author distinguishes between two types of composite definitions: hadd, which reveals the essence, and rasm, which distinguishes the thing from others. Let's unpack this step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reason for composition
The passage states that a definer requires 'conceiving the establishment of something for something' – a predicative relation. This forces composition; you cannot define with a single term.
2
Understand the role of the mental clue (qareena)
A 'qareena aqliyya' is needed to validate the transition from the definiens to the definiendum. Without it, the composite would not yield the intended concept.
3
Recognize that even simple terms are composite
The author shows that terms like 'rational' (natiq) actually mean 'a thing having speech' – composite. This underscores that all definiens are composite.
4
Distinguish hadd from rasm
Hadd explains the quiddity (kunh) of a thing, e.g., 'man is a rational animal'. Rasm distinguishes the thing from others using an accident, e.g., 'man is a laughing animal'.
5
Apply the concept to a concrete example
For 'man', the hadd is 'rational animal' (genus + essential differentia), while the rasm is 'laughing animal' (genus + accidental property). Both are composite, but one reveals essence, the other merely distinguishes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَلْ؛ لأنَّ المعرف لا بُدَّ فيه من تصورِ ثبوت شيءٍ لشيءٍ، فَيَكُونُ مُرَكَّبًا ( ) وهذا معنى قولهم لا بُدَّ فيه من قريْنَةٍ عقلية مصححةٍ للانتقال (۲)؛ ولهذا (۳) قالوا: معنى النَّاطِقِ شَيء لَهُ النُّطْقُ)، ومعنى الضَّاحِكِ: شيءٌ لَهُ الضَّحْكُ. وَإِنَّما سُمي شارِحَاً؛ لشرْحِهِ الماهية: إما بكُنْههَا وهو الحد، أو بوَجْهِ يميرُها عَمّا عَدَاها - وهوَ الرسم. (۱) التعريف بالمركب ضروري لأنَّ تصور الإنسان يكون بالمعرف والمعرف لا بد فيه من تصور شيء لشيء مثل: الإنسان حيوان ناطق، لا بد من تصور شيء هو ناطق لشيء هو الحيوان، ومن ثم ستعرف الإنسان. (۲) لو عرفنا الإنسان بالحيوان وحده وبالناطق وحده، فإنا لا نصل إلى حقيقة الإنسان وماهيته فلا بدَّ من قرينة والقرينة هي ثبوت الناطق للحيوان لأجل أن نصل إلى أن الإنسان حيوان ناطق؛ لأن الحيوان عام أعم من الإنسان، والناطق خاص ولا يدل وجود العام على الخاص إلا بقرينة وهي أن تثبت الخاص الناطق للعام وهو الحيوان، وباجتماعهما نصل إلى حقيقة الإنسان. (۳) أي: ولأجل حصول القرينة. (٤) أي: من عرف الإنسان بالناطق اضطر إلى تقدير جزء آخر ليتم التركيب، فيقدر قبل ناطق شيء ناطق إذا أراد تعريفه بذاته، ويقدر شيء ضاحك إن أراد تمييزه عن المشاركات، فالأول هو الحد والثاني هو الرسم. لذا سمي التعريف شارحاً، أي: مفسّراً للذات، وهو الحد، أو يميز عن المشاركات وهو الرسم.
Rather, because the definer necessarily requires conceiving the establishment of something for something, so it becomes composite. And this is the meaning of their statement: a mental clue (qareena) is necessary in it to validate the transition. Hence they said: the meaning of 'rational' (natiq) is 'a thing having speech', and the meaning of 'laughing' (dahik) is 'a thing having laughter'. And it is called explanatory (sharih) only because it explains the essence: either by its quiddity (kunh) and that is the essential definition (hadd), or by an aspect that distinguishes it from others—and that is the descriptive definition (rasm). (1) Definition by a composite is necessary because the conception of man occurs through the definer, and the definer necessarily requires conceiving something for something, such as 'man is a rational animal': one must conceive something which is rational for something which is animal, and thereby one comes to know man. (2) If we define man by 'animal' alone or by 'rational' alone, we do not attain the reality and quiddity of man; so a clue is necessary, and the clue is the establishment of 'rational' for 'animal' in order to arrive at 'man is a rational animal', because 'animal' is more general than 'man', and 'rational' is specific; the existence of the universal does not indicate the specific except with a clue, which is to establish the specific (rational) for the universal (animal); by their combination we arrive at the reality of man. (3) That is: for the sake of obtaining the clue. (4) That is: whoever defines man as 'rational' is compelled to posit another part to complete the composition; he posits before 'rational' a 'thing rational' if he intends definition by essence, and he posits 'a thing laughing' if he intends distinction from partners—the first is the essential definition (hadd), the second is the descriptive definition (rasm). Hence the definition is called explanatory (sharih), i.e., an interpreter of the essence—that is the hadd—or a distinguisher from partners—that is the rasm.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage explains why every definition must be composite and then distinguishes between hadd (essential) and rasm (descriptive) definition. The author begins by stating the reason: a definer (mu'arraf) requires conceiving the 'establishment of something for something' (thubut shay'in li shay'in), meaning we must predicate one concept of another, hence composition is necessary. This is what logicians mean by requiring a 'mental clue' (qareena 'aqliyya) that validates the mental transition from the definiens to the definiendum. For example, the term 'natiq' (rational) is not a simple idea; its meaning is 'a thing having speech' (shay'un lahu al-nutq), which is composite. Similarly, 'dahik' (laughing) means 'a thing having laughter' (shay'un lahu al-dahk). The passage then explains why such a definition is called 'sharih' (explanatory): it explains the essence either by its quiddity (kunh) — that is the hadd — or by a distinguishing aspect — that is the rasm. The footnotes elaborate: definition by composite is necessary because to conceive 'man' we must combine 'animal' and 'rational' in a predicative relation. If we only say 'animal' or only say 'rational', we do not get the full definition; a clue (qareena) is needed to connect the specific to the general. The footnote (4) shows that if one tries to define man by 'rational' alone, one implicitly posits a 'thing' to complete the composition: 'thing rational' for hadd, or 'thing laughing' for rasm. Thus, the definition is always composite and explanatory either of the essence or of the distinguishability.

Solving Infinite Regress in Defining the Definer
The author examines the objection that defining the definer leads to infinite regress and provides two valid solutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the problem of infinite regress in defining the definer and apply the two valid solutions (self-evidence of parts and accidental applicability) to avoid it.

Core Idea
Defining the definer does not lead to infinite regress if its parts are self-evident or already known.

In logic, definitions cannot go on forever. The objection that defining the definer leads to an infinite regress threatens the very possibility of definition. But logicians have a response, as shown in this passage from al-Fanari's commentary. This lesson walks you through the problem and its logical solutions—foundational for understanding how definitions ground knowledge in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the Objection
Identify the claim: 'If the definer had a definer, infinite regress would follow, which is impossible, so defining the definer is not allowed.' This is a conditional argument: P → Q, ¬Q, therefore ¬P (modus tollens).
The objection uses the impossibility of tasalsul (infinite regress).
2
Recognize the Rejected Solution
The text rejects the answer that the definer of the defined is identical to the defined itself (e.g., 'existence of existence'). Identity (ayniyya) is not admitted—it would collapse the distinction between definer and defined.
This shows that not every attempted solution is valid; logical rigor requires rejecting false equivalences.
3
Analyse the First Valid Solution
The parts of the definer (e.g., genus and differentia in a composite definition) are either self-evident (badihi) or already known from prior knowledge (e.g., through divine revelation or a teacher). Thus they need no further definition, stopping the regress.
Self-evidence means the concept is immediately grasped, like 'existence' or 'non-existence'.
4
Analyse the Second Valid Solution
Even when these parts function as a definer for another thing (the 'limited' or defined), they are known by an accidental property (aarid)—namely, that they truly apply to the limited. This accidental status does not create a need for a new definition.
The accident is the truth of 'the absolute definer of the limited thing' being predicated of the parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قِيلَ : لا يَجوزُ تعريفُ المعرِّف؛ لأنَّهُ لو كانَ للمعرِّفِ مُعَرِّفُ لزِمَ التَّسَلْسُلُ. لا يُجاب عنه بأنَّ معرّف المعرَّفِ عَيْنُهُ كوجود الوُجودِ، لأنّ العَيْنِيَّةَ مَمْنوعَةٌ. بَلْ يُجَابُ: إِمَا بِأَنَّ التَّسَلسُلَ غَيْرُ لازم؛ لأنَّ معرّف المعرِّفِ من حيث هو غير محتاج إلى معرف آخر. إمّا لبدَاهَةِ أجزائه، أو لكونها معلومة. فكما أَنَّهُ من حيثُ هو غَيْرُ محتاج إلى مُعَرِّفِ آخرَ كذلك لا يَحتاجُ إليه من حيث هو معرّفٌ؛ لكونِهِ مَعْلُوما باعتبارِ عَارِض: وهُوَ صِدْقُ مُطلَقِ المُعرِّف المحدودِ عليه.
It was said: It is not permissible to define the definer, because if the definer had a definer, infinite regress would follow. It is not answered by [saying] that the definer of the defined is identical to it, like the existence of existence, because identity is rejected. Rather, it is answered: either by [saying] that regress is not necessary, because the definer of the definer, as such, does not need another definer, either due to the self-evidence of its parts or because they are known. So just as it, as such, does not need another definer, likewise it does not need it as a definer, because it is known by virtue of an accident, which is the truth of the absolute definer of the limited [thing] being applied to it.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage refines the logical rule that the definer (mu'arraf) cannot itself be defined. First, an objection is raised: if the definer required a definer, there would be infinite regress (tasalsul), which is unacceptable. The author then rejects a weak counterargument—that the definer of the defined is identical to the defined (e.g., 'existence of existence')—since identity is not admitted in such cases. Then two valid answers are given. The first: regress is not necessary because the parts of the definer (e.g., 'animal' and 'rational' in 'rational animal') are either self-evident (badihi) or already known from prior knowledge (e.g., through divine inspiration or a teacher). Thus, as a composite, it needs no further definer. The second: even when these parts become a definer for another thing (the 'limited' or defined), they remain known by an accidental property (aarid)—namely, that they truly apply to the defined. This accidental property does not create a need for a new definition. The passage thus preserves the possibility of definition without circularity or infinite regress.

التَّسَلْسُل
العَيْنِيَّة
البَدَاهَة
العَارِض

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because infinite regress is accepted in logic as harmless.
D) Because the definer is defined by its opposite, which is known directly.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress in definition is avoided when the parts of the definer are either self-evident or already known, or when the definer's applicability is an accidental property of the defined.

Resolving the Regress Objection and Distinguishing Hadd from Rasm
Author gives two answers to regress, then defines hadd and rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the two solutions to the infinite regress objection in definitions and classify any given definition as either hadd or rasm.

Core Idea
A finite definition is possible because its parts are known or because definitions are conventional.

In this passage, the author tackles a classic objection: if every definiendum requires a definer, then every definer itself requires a definer, leading to an infinite regress. The author offers two distinct solutions—one epistemological and one conventional—and then clarifies that all definitions are either essential (hadd) or accidental (rasm). Understanding this distinction is critical for rigorous concept formation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the regress objection
The objection states that definitions lead to infinite regress. Find how the author sets up the problem: every definer itself needs a definer, ad infinitum.
2
Analyze the first answer: epistemological sufficiency
The definer's parts are self-evident or already known, so the regress is not necessary. Understand why the definer as such needs no further definition.
First answer: 'التسلسل غير لازم' because the parts are بديهية or معلومة.
3
Analyze the second answer: conventional termination
Definitions are conventional (اعتبارية); when the consideration stops, the regress stops. This is a pragmatic solution.
Second answer: 'التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية غير محال'.
4
Apply the hadd/rasm criteria
Given an explanatory statement, determine if it consists solely of essential attributes (ذاتيات) — then it is hadd; if it includes accidental attributes, it is rasm.
القول الشارح: إما حد أو رسم.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَلْ يُجَابُ: إِمَا بِأَنَّ التَّسَلسُلَ غَيْرُ لازم؛ لأنَّ معرّف المعرِّفِ من حيث هو غير محتاج إلى معرف آخر . إمّا لبدَاهَةِ أجزائه، أو لكونها معلومة. فكما أَنَّهُ من حيثُ هُوَ غَيْرُ محتاج إلى مُعَرِّفِ آخرَ كذلك لا يَحتاجُ إليه من حيث هو معرّفٌ؛ لكونِهِ مَعْلُوما باعتبارِ عَارِض: وهو صِدْقُ مُطلَقِ المُعرِّف المحدودِ عليه. وقد عَرَفْت أنَّ الخاصَّ يَقَعُ معرفا باعتبار غير اعتبارِ خُصوصِيَّتِهِ. وإمَّا: بأنَّ التَسَلْسُلَ في الأُمُورِ الاعتبارية؛ لانقطاعه بانقطاع الاعتبار غيرُ مُحَالٍ . فعُلِمَ أَنَّ القَولَ الشارِحَ إِمَّا حَدٌ أَو رَسْمٌ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ بِمُجَرَّدِ الذاتِيَّاتِ فَحَدُّ، وإلا فَرَسْمُ.
Rather, it is answered: either that the regress is not necessary, because the definer of the definer, as such, does not need another definer; either due to the self-evidence of its parts, or because they are known. So just as it, as such, does not need another definer, similarly it does not need it as a definer, because it is known by virtue of an accident: which is the truth of the absolute definer of the limited. And you have known that the specific occurs as a definer by virtue of other than its specificity. Or that the regress in conventional matters is not impossible due to its termination by the termination of the consideration. So it is known that the explanatory statement is either a definition or a description; because if it is by the essential attributes alone, it is a definition, otherwise it is a description.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage addresses a critical objection to definitions: that every definition requires a prior definition, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The commentator gives two answers. First, the regress is not necessary because the parts of a definition (like 'animal' and 'rational') are either self-evident (badihiyya) or already known from prior learning. The definer of the definer, considered in itself, does not need another definer; and when it becomes a definer, it remains known due to the accident of being a definer. The second answer is that such definitions are conventional (i'tibariyya) matters; the regress can be cut off simply by ceasing the consideration. Thus, the regress is not impossible because it depends on human convention. The passage then concludes that an explanatory statement (qawl sharh) is either a hadd (definition) if composed solely of essential attributes (dhatiyyat), or a rasm (description) if it includes accidental attributes (aradiyyat). This classification is fundamental in logic: a hadd gives the essence, a rasm gives a distinguishing mark. The examples follow the Islamic tradition: e.g., 'human is a rational animal' is a hadd, while 'human is a laughing animal' is a rasm.

Key Terms
التسلسل
المعرف
الذاتيات
الحد
الرسم

Key Takeaway
The regress of definitions is avoided either because the parts of a definition are known or because definitions are conventional; this allows definitions to be finite and foundational.

Complete vs Incomplete Definitions (Hadd Tamm vs Naqis)
Author defines hadd and introduces its complete and incomplete forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given definition as either complete (hadd tamm) or incomplete (hadd naqis) by identifying the genus as near or remote.

Core Idea
A complete definition uses the near genus; an incomplete uses a remote genus.

In Islamic logic, a definition (hadd) must capture the quiddity of a thing. But not all definitions are equal: some include only partial essential properties. This lesson teaches you to spot the difference by analysing the genus—is it the nearest category or a more distant one?

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definiendum (the thing being defined) in the passage. In the example, it is 'insan' (man).
2
Identify the genus (jins)
Find the category that answers 'what is it?' and is broader than the definiendum. For man, the genus could be 'animal' (hayawan) or 'body' (jism).
3
Determine if the genus is near or remote
Check how many species share the genus. Animal is the near genus because fewer species share it; body is remote because more species (including plants and inanimates) share it.
The passage explains that near genus has fewer participants.
4
Identify the differentia (fasl)
Find the distinguishing property that separates this species from others in the same genus. For man, it is 'rational' (natiq).
5
Classify the definition
If the definition uses the near genus plus the differentia (e.g., 'rational animal'), it is complete (hadd tamm). If it uses a remote genus plus the differentia (e.g., 'rational body'), it is incomplete (hadd naqis).

(۳) إذا قلنا الإنسان شيء ناطق، فالشيئية أي الوجود عارضة له إذن التعريف به يسمى رسماً. وقد سبق أن وَضَحْنا ذلك في ص ٦٣ .
He defined (al-hadd) as a statement indicating the quiddity of a thing. If it is a definition by the totality of the essential properties, it is a complete definition. If it is by some of them, it is incomplete. So it is a definition (hadd) because it prevents the entry of others into it, and al-hadd in language is prevention. Its completeness and incompleteness are according to the essential properties. So the complete definition is that which is composed of the near genus of a thing and its differentia, like 'rational animal' in relation to man. Hence he said: that is the complete definition. The incomplete definition is that which is composed of the remote genus and the near differentia, like 'rational body' in relation to man. (Marginal notes: 1) The author defined hadd as he will mention later. (2) Al-hadd is the separator between two things. (3) Because it distinguishes the essential properties of the defined, separating it from the rest of the participants. (4) The essential properties of man are animality and rationality; animality is the near genus of man because those sharing it with him are fewer than those sharing body; because animality is what has life, and body is because man is a body, but more share body than animality as it includes inanimate objects and plants; therefore if the remote genus is brought, the definition is incomplete. And he did not say 'or by its differentia only' like 'rational' in defining man, as they claimed, because the differentia is composite in meaning, and consideration is for meanings. If its meaning is 'a body or substance having rationality and the like', it is like 'rational body' itself. If its meaning is 'a thing having rationality and the like', it is not a definition because thingness is accidental. (Marginal notes: 1) If we say 'man is rational', the word 'rational' is an adjective for an omitted described [noun] estimated either as 'rational body' or 'rational substance'. (2) That is, an incomplete genus; because man, as he is an animal, is also a body and substance. Here is clarification of man in relation to his genus and differentia: Genera: Jawhar (substance) remote genus of man, having position (mutaḥayyiz); Man is a tamm body, middle genus, moving; Hayawān (animal) near genus, nāṭiq (rational); Man a species of animal; Zayd an individual of man. (3) If we say 'man is a rational thing', thingness i.e. existence is accidental to him, so the definition by it is called a rasm. We have already clarified that on p. 63.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage defines the logical term 'hadd' (definition) and divides it into complete and incomplete. The author begins by stating the definition: 'hadd is a statement indicating the quiddity (kunh) of a thing.' He then explains that if the definition comprises all the essential properties (dhatiyyat), it is a complete definition (hadd tamm); if only some, it is incomplete (hadd naqis). The term 'hadd' itself is derived from the linguistic meaning of prevention, because a proper definition prevents non-essentials from entering the definition of a thing. The completeness or incompleteness is based on whether the definition includes the near genus (jins qarib) and the differentia (fasl), or a remote genus (jins ba'id) with the differentia. The example given: for man, 'rational animal' is a complete definition (near genus + differentia), while 'rational body' is incomplete (remote genus + differentia). The author then addresses why one cannot define by only the differentia (e.g., just 'rational' for man). He explains that 'natiq' (rational) is itself composite in meaning. If by 'rational' one means 'a body or substance possessing rationality', then it essentially equals 'rational body' or 'rational substance', which is an incomplete definition. If one means 'a thing possessing rationality', then 'thingness' is accidental, so it cannot be a true definition (hadd) but rather a description (rasm). The marginal notes further clarify the hierarchy of genera for man: substance (remote genus), body (middle genus), animal (near genus), with differentia 'rational'. The discussion ensures that the student understands the correct structure and limits of definition in logic.

Why Defining by Differentia Alone Fails
Author explains why defining by only differentia is insufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proposed definition using only a differentia (like 'rational') is actually an incomplete definition or a mere description (rasm).

Core Idea
A differentia alone is composite in meaning; using it yields either an incomplete hadd or a rasm.

You might think that saying 'man is rational' is a sufficient definition. But the logicians show that the differentia 'rational' carries hidden assumptions. This lesson dissects that claim: why 'natiq' alone fails to be a true definition, and what it actually becomes.

Guided Path
1
Extract the proposed definition using only differentia
The author considers the case where someone defines 'man' simply as 'rational' (natiq). Identify this as a one-word definition.
2
Analyze the meaning of the differentia
The word 'rational' (natiq) is an adjective that implies an implicit subject. What is that subject? It could be 'body', 'substance', or 'thing'. The passage says natiq is composite in meaning.
The marginal notes explain that 'natiq' is an adjective for an omitted described noun.
3
Check the two possible interpretations
If 'rational' means 'a body or substance having rationality', then the definition becomes 'rational body' or 'rational substance' – which is an incomplete definition (hadd naqis) using remote genus. If it means 'a thing having rationality', then 'thingness' (shay'iyya) is accidental, so the definition becomes a rasm.
The passage: 'If its meaning is a body or substance... it is like rational body itself. If its meaning is a thing... it is not a definition because thingness is accidental.'
4
Determine the outcome
In either case, the definition is not a complete hadd (hadd tamm). It is either incomplete (naqis) or a mere description (rasm). Therefore, a single differentia cannot stand alone as a proper definition.

He defined (al-hadd) as a statement indicating the quiddity of a thing. If it is a definition by the totality of the essential properties, it is a complete definition. If it is by some of them, it is incomplete. So it is a definition (hadd) because it prevents the entry of others into it, and al-hadd in language is prevention. Its completeness and incompleteness are according to the essential properties. So the complete definition is that which is composed of the near genus of a thing and its differentia, like 'rational animal' in relation to man. Hence he said: that is the complete definition. The incomplete definition is that which is composed of the remote genus and the near differentia, like 'rational body' in relation to man. (Marginal notes: 1) The author defined hadd as he will mention later. (2) Al-hadd is the separator between two things. (3) Because it distinguishes the essential properties of the defined, separating it from the rest of the participants. (4) The essential properties of man are animality and rationality; animality is the near genus of man because those sharing it with him are fewer than those sharing body; because animality is what has life, and body is because man is a body, but more share body than animality as it includes inanimate objects and plants; therefore if the remote genus is brought, the definition is incomplete. And he did not say 'or by its differentia only' like 'rational' in defining man, as they claimed, because the differentia is composite in meaning, and consideration is for meanings. If its meaning is 'a body or substance having rationality and the like', it is like 'rational body' itself. If its meaning is 'a thing having rationality and the like', it is not a definition because thingness is accidental. (Marginal notes: 1) If we say 'man is rational', the word 'rational' is an adjective for an omitted described [noun] estimated either as 'rational body' or 'rational substance'. (2) That is, an incomplete genus; because man, as he is an animal, is also a body and substance. Here is clarification of man in relation to his genus and differentia: Genera: Jawhar (substance) remote genus of man, having position (mutaḥayyiz); Man is a tamm body, middle genus, moving; Hayawān (animal) near genus, nāṭiq (rational); Man a species of animal; Zayd an individual of man. (3) If we say 'man is a rational thing', thingness i.e. existence is accidental to him, so the definition by it is called a rasm. We have already clarified that on p. 63.

Why Natiq Alone Is Not a Complete Definition
Author explains why 'natiq' alone is insufficient for hadd due to its composite meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a composite term like 'natiq' cannot serve as a proper differentia in a hadd definition, and distinguish between hadd and rasm.

Core Idea
A term that implicitly includes a genus cannot be a complete differentia—it becomes a rasm or a tautology.

In this passage, the author of Fawaid al-Fanari explains why the term 'nāṭiq' (speaking) cannot simply be used as the differentia in the definition of man. Although some logicians present 'nāṭiq' as the differentia, the author shows that 'nāṭiq' is composite in meaning—it secretly refers to a substance or body that speaks. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing correct definitions (ḥadd) in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term in question
The term 'nāṭiq' is proposed as the differentia for man. Note that it is a single word, but the author argues it is not simple.
2
Analyze the composition of 'nāṭiq'
The author states that 'nāṭiq' is composite because it carries an implicit subject: either 'body having speech' or 'substance having speech'. This means it already contains a genus.
If the meaning is 'body that speaks', then the differentia includes the genus 'body', making the definition circular.
3
Examine the two interpretations
If 'nāṭiq' means 'body having speech', the definition 'man is a speaking body' reduces to a species of body, not a proper definition. If it means 'thing having speech', then 'thingness' is accidental, turning the definition into a rasm (description).
4
Conclude the logical fault
A true differentia must be simple and not implicitly contain a genus. 'Nāṭiq' fails this test, so it cannot be used alone as the differentia in a hadd.

Source Text

If its meaning is 'a body having speech' or 'a substance having speech' and the like, it would be like 'the speaking body' itself. And if its meaning is 'a thing having speech' and the like, it would not be a definition (hadd), because 'thingness' (shay'iyya) is accidental. Commentary
This passage explains why the term 'natiq' (speaking) cannot simply be used as the differentia (fadl) in the definition of man without further analysis. The author argues that 'natiq' is composite in meaning; it implicitly carries a subject (e.g., body or substance). If its meaning is 'body having speech' or 'substance having speech', then the definition reduces to 'body that speaks' or 'substance that speaks', which is not a proper definition because it uses a genus (body or substance) plus a property, not a true differentia. If its meaning is 'thing having speech', then 'thingness' is an accidental attribute, so the definition becomes a mere description (rasm), not a true essential definition (hadd). The footnotes elaborate: in 'man is natiq', 'natiq' is an adjective for an implicit subject (body or substance). This shows that 'natiq' is not a simple differentia but includes a genus. The diagram in footnote (2) illustrates the hierarchy: substance (receptive), perfect body (mobile), animal (natiq), man (species), Zayd (individual). This explains why 'natiq' alone, if interpreted as a thing with speech, yields a definition using the accidental 'thingness', which is a rasm. Thus, the passage guards against the misunderstanding that a single term like 'natiq' can serve as a complete differentia without unpacking its composition.

Hadd and Rasm

Complete and Incomplete Rasm (Descriptive Definition)
Author defines rasm and divides it into two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a complete rasm (al-rasm al-tāmm) and an incomplete rasm (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ) by identifying the genus and properties used in each.

Core Idea
A complete rasm uses the proximate genus plus necessary properties; an incomplete rasm lacks one or both.

In the study of definitions, logicians distinguish between essential definitions (hadd) and descriptive definitions (rasm). While hadd captures the essence through genus and differentia, rasm identifies the thing by its properties and genus. This passage from the Fawaid al-Fanari explains the two kinds of rasm—complete and incomplete—and gives the classic example of 'laughing animal' for man. Understanding this distinction helps you evaluate how precisely a thing is being described.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two divisions of rasm
Read the first line: 'والرسم أيضا قسمان: تام وناقص' (And rasm is also two types: complete and incomplete). The author immediately divides rasm into two categories.
This is a typical logical division.
2
Identify the condition for complete rasm
Look for the condition: 'إن كان جنسا قريباً مقيّداً بما يخصصه' (if it is a proximate genus qualified by what specifies it). That means the complete rasm must include two elements: a proximate genus and a specifying attribute (a necessary property, khāṣṣa).
The property must be necessary, not accidental.
3
Analyze the example 'laughing animal'
The passage gives 'الحيوان الضاحك' (laughing animal) as a complete rasm for man. Here 'animal' is the proximate genus, and 'laughing' is a necessary property (الخاصة). Check that it includes all humans and excludes non-humans.
Laughter in potentiality is always present in humans.
4
Contrast incomplete rasm
The incomplete rasm either uses a remote genus (like 'body' for man) or omits the genus entirely, relying on a set of accidents that collectively apply to one reality. For example, 'a substance that laughs' is incomplete because it uses 'substance' which is a remote genus.
The totality of accidents may still identify the thing, but the definition is less precise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف الرسم وأقسامه]
والرسم أيضا قسمان: تام وناقص؛
لأنَّ المذكور فيه إن كان جنسا قريباً مقيّداً بما يخصصه فتام؛
لكونه أثراً يُسمَّى رَسْما .
ولكونه مشابهاً بالحدّ التام في ذلك يُسمى تاما .
وإن لم يكن كذلِكَ فناقِصُ ؛ لنقصانه عن تلك التمامية. فالرسم التام هو الذي يتركَّبُ من جنس الشَّيءِ القَرِيبِ وَخَواصّهِ اللازمة كالحيوان الضاحك في تعريف الإنسان. والرسمُ الناقِصُ : وهو الذي يتركَّبُ عن عَرَضيَّاتٍ تختَصُّ جملتها بحقيقة واحدة سواءٌ لم يختص شيء من آحادها، أو
(۱) مثل الحيوان الضاحك بالنسبة للإنسان؛ لأن الضحك من خواصه.
(۲) لكونه: أي لكون الرسم.
(۳) أي: علامة. (٤) أي : شابة الحد التام؛ لأنه ركب من الجنس القريب - والحد التام ركب منه - ومن الفصل، وهذا ركب من الجنس القريب والخاصة. (٥) أي: لم يركب من الجنس القريب، بل من الجنس البعيد مثل: الجسم. أو لم يذكر الجنس أصلاً فإنه سيكون ناقصاً.
(٦) فإنَّ الضحك بالقوة ملازم للإنسان، أو بالفعل فإنَّه غير ملازم له. (۷) مثل الضاحك والمتعجب.
[Definition of rasm and its divisions] And rasm is also two types: complete (tam) and incomplete (naqis). Because if what is mentioned in it is a proximate genus qualified by what specifies it, then it is complete; because it is a trace (athar) called rasm. And because it resembles the complete hadd in that, it is called complete. And if it is not like that, then it is incomplete, due to its deficiency from that completeness. The complete rasm is that which is composed of the proximate genus of the thing and its necessary properties, like 'laughing animal' in the definition of man. The incomplete rasm is that which is composed of accidents whose totality is specific to one reality, whether no single one of them is specific, or [the sentence cuts off]. (1) Like 'laughing animal' with respect to man, because laughter is among his properties. (2) 'Because it is': i.e., because the rasm. (3) i.e., a sign. (4) i.e., resembling the complete hadd; because the complete hadd is composed of proximate genus and differentia, while this is composed of proximate genus and proper attribute. (5) i.e., not composed of proximate genus, but of remote genus like 'body', or the genus is not mentioned at all, then it will be incomplete. (6) For laughter in potentiality is necessary for man, but in actuality it is not necessary. (7) Like 'the one who laughs' and 'the one who wonders'.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines rasm (descriptive definition) and divides it into two types: complete (tam) and incomplete (naqis). The definition is given: if the rasm includes a proximate genus qualified by what specifies it (i.e., a necessary property), it is complete; otherwise it is incomplete. The author explains that the complete rasm is called 'tam' because it is an 'athar' (trace) and because it resembles the complete hadd (essential definition) in using the proximate genus. The incomplete rasm lacks this completeness. The passage then gives examples: 'laughing animal' for man is a complete rasm because it uses the proximate genus 'animal' and the necessary property 'laughing' (laughter is a property peculiar to humans). The incomplete rasm is composed of accidents that collectively apply to one reality, but no single accident may be exclusive, or the genus may be remote or omitted. Footnotes clarify: laughter is a property (khāṣṣa) as in example (1). Notes (2)-(4) explain the terms: 'athar' means sign, and the resemblance to hadd is due to composition from genus and property. Note (5) specifies that if a remote genus like 'body' is used, or no genus at all, that is incomplete. Note (6) distinguishes potential laughter (always present) from actual laughter (not always). Note (7) gives examples of properties like 'the one who laughs' and 'the one who wonders' as individual accidents that may not be exclusive.

Key Takeaway
A complete rasm accurately identifies a species using its proximate genus and necessary property, while an incomplete rasm falls short by omitting the genus or using non-exclusive accidents.

Distinguishing Complete and Incomplete Rasm
Author defines complete and incomplete rasm with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given description as a complete rasm (rasm tamm) or incomplete rasm (rasm naqis) and justify your classification based on its components.

Core Idea
A complete rasm uses a genus plus a necessary property; an incomplete rasm uses multiple accidents.

In this passage, the author breaks down the definition by property (rasm) into two types: complete and incomplete. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic logic, especially when a full essential definition (hadd) is not available. The complete rasm resembles an essential definition in structure, while the incomplete rasm uses a collection of accidents. We will examine the criteria and examples to learn how to identify each type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition's components
Look at the description given. Does it contain a genus (like 'animal') or only accidental properties (like 'walks on two feet')?
2
Check for a near genus
If a genus is present, determine if it is the immediate genus (jins qarib). For example, 'animal' is the immediate genus for man.
3
Look for a necessary property (khassa laazima)
Is there a property that is peculiar to the species and always accompanies it, but is not part of its essence? For man, 'laughing' is such a property.
If both a near genus and a necessary property are present, it's a complete rasm.
4
If no genus or no peculiar property, classify as incomplete rasm
If the definition lacks a genus or the property is not exclusive, it is incomplete. The example 'walking on two feet, broad-nailed, bare-skinned, upright' uses four accidents that together specify man, but none alone is exclusive.
Even if the last attribute is exclusive, the definition is still incomplete if it lacks a genus.
5
Test with provided examples
Apply the above steps to 'animal that laughs' (rasm tamm) and 'walking on two feet, broad-nailed, bare-skinned, upright' (rasm naqis). Verify the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرسم أيضا قسمان: تام وناقص؛ لأنَّ المذكور فيه إن كان جنسا قريباً مقيّداً بما يخصصه فتام؛ لكونه أثراً يُسمَّى رَسْماً. ولكونه مشابهاً بالحدّ التام في ذلك يُسمى تاما. وإن لم يكن كذلِكَ فناقِصُ؛ لنقصانه عن تلك التمامية. فالرسم التام هو الذي يتركَّبُ من جنس الشَّيءِ القَرِيبِ وخصاصه اللازمة كالحيوان الضاحك في تعريف الإنسان. والرسمُ الناقِصُ: وهو الذي يتركَّبُ عن عَرَضيَّاتٍ تختَصُّ جملتها بحقيقة واحدة سواءٌ لم يختص شيء من آحادها، أو اختصَّتْ الواحِدَةُ الأخيرة. كقولنا في تعريف الإنسان إنَّه ماشي على قدميه، يخرجُ الماشي على الأقدام الأربعةِ عَريضُ الأَظْفَارِ يُخرِجُ مُدوّرَ الأظفار كالطيور بادِي البشرةِ يخرجُ مستور البشرةِ بالشعر مُسْتَقيمُ القامَةِ يخرجُ منحرفُ القامة. فكلّ من الأوصافِ الأربعةِ يُوجَدُ في غير الإنسان. فلما قال: ضحاك بالطَّبْعِ خرج غيرُه. ولا يَرِدُ: ما يُقال: مِنْ أَنَّ بعضَها غنيَّةٌ عَنِ البَعْضِ، فَإِنَّ ذلك غيرُ
And the [definition by] description (rasm) is also two types: complete and incomplete. Because if what is mentioned in it is a near genus restricted by what specifies it, then it is complete; because it is an effect called 'rasm'. And because it resembles the complete definition (hadd tamm) in that, it is called complete. If it is not like that, then it is incomplete; due to its deficiency from that completeness. So the complete description (rasm tamm) is that which is composed of the near genus of the thing and its necessary properties, like 'animal that laughs' in the definition of man. And the incomplete description (rasm naqis): it is that which is composed of accidental properties whose totality is specific to a single reality, whether no single one of them is specific, or the last one alone is specific. Like our saying in defining man: 'he is one who walks on two feet' — this excludes those who walk on four feet; 'broad-nailed' — excludes rounded-nailed like birds; 'bare-skinned' — excludes those with skin covered by hair; 'upright in stature' — excludes those with crooked stature. So each of the four attributes is found in other than man. Then when he said: 'laughing by nature', it excludes others. And it is not objected: what is said that some of them are dispensable for others, for that is not [valid].
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of definition by description (rasm): complete (tamm) and incomplete (naqis). The author begins by stating the division, then explains the criterion: if the description uses the near genus qualified by a specifying property, it is complete — because it is an effect (athar) that gives recognition and resembles the complete definition (hadd tamm) in having both genus and a distinguishing element. If not, it is incomplete. The complete rasm is exemplified by 'animal that laughs' for man, where 'animal' is the near genus and 'laughing' is a necessary property (khassa laazima). The incomplete rasm is composed of accidental properties (aradiyyat) that collectively belong to a single reality, either with none of them being exclusive individually, or with only the last one being exclusive. The author gives an example for man: 'walking on two feet' excludes quadrupeds; 'broad-nailed' excludes birds; 'bare-skinned' excludes furred animals; 'upright stature' excludes bent-bodied animals. Each of these four attributes is found in non-human beings, but together they narrow the definition. Finally, when 'laughing by nature' is added (as in 'laughing by nature'), it becomes a complete rasm because it is a peculiar property. The author also addresses a potential objection: that some of these attributes are redundant. He rejects this objection, implying that the combination is necessary for a precise definition. The passage thus clarifies the construction and validity conditions for both types of rasm.

رسم ناقص
جنس قريب
خاصة لازمة

Complete Rasm: Human as an Example
Author uses the definition of human to illustrate complete rasm with multiple attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a complete rasm and explain why the combination of attributes uniquely distinguishes the definiendum.

Core Idea
A complete rasm uses a set of accidentals that collectively exclude all non-instances.

In this passage, the author illustrates the concept of a complete rasm (a definition by accidental properties) by analyzing the common definition of 'human'. He lists four physical attributes—walking on two feet, broad nails, bare skin, and straight stature—each of which individually applies to some non-humans, but together they still fail to uniquely identify human until he adds the property 'laughing by nature', which is exclusive to humans. This example clarifies how a complete rasm works: it is a collection of distinguishing properties that, when combined, are found only in the definiendum, even if each property alone is not exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five attributes used in the rasm
From the Arabic, list the five descriptors: mashī ʿalā rijlayn (walker on two feet), ʿarīḍ al-aẓfār (broad-nailed), bādī al-bashara (bare-skinned), mustaqīm al-qāma (straight-statured), and ḍaḥḥāk bi al-ṭabʿ (laughing by nature).
The first four are physical, the last is behavioral.
2
Check each attribute for exclusivity
For each of the first four, think of non-human beings that share that attribute. For example, birds walk on two feet but have round nails; bears have broad nails but are not bare-skinned; etc. The passage explicitly states each is found in other than human.
This shows that no single attribute suffices.
3
Test the combination of all five
Now consider the set of all five attributes. Is there any being other than human that possesses all five? The answer is no, especially because 'laughing by nature' is unique to humans. Therefore the combination is uniquely identifying, making it a complete rasm.
The last attribute is the differentia that finalizes the exclusion.
4
Address the redundancy objection
The author anticipates that some attributes may be logically redundant (e.g., bare skin might imply straight stature). He responds that redundancy is not required to be avoided; the purpose is merely to give an example that illustrates the concept of a complete rasm.
In a rasm, accidental properties need not be independent; only the collective uniqueness matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختصَّتْ الواحِدَةُ الأخيرة. كقولنا في تعريف الإنسان إنَّه ماشي على قدميه، يخرج الماشي على الأقدام الأربعة عريض الأظفار يخرج مدور الأظفار كالطيور بادي البشرة يخرج مستور البشرة بالشعر مستقيم القامة يخرج منحرف القامة. فكل من الأوصاف الأربعة يوجد في غير الإنسان. فلما قال: ضحاك بالطبع خرج غيره. ولا يرد: ما يقال: من أن بعضها غنية عن البعض، فإن ذلك غير ملتزم، والغرض التمثيل.
The last one is specific. As our saying in defining human: he is a walker on two feet, exits the walker on four feet; broad-nailed, exits the round-nailed like birds; bare-skinned, exits the skin-covered with hair; straight-statured, exits the crooked-statured. So each of the four attributes is found in other than human. Then when he said: 'laughing by nature', others exited. And it is not objected: what is said that some of them are independent of others, for that is not necessary, and the purpose is exemplification.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of complete rasm (definition by accidental properties) using the example of defining 'human' (insān). The commentator lists four attributes: walking on two feet, broad nails, bare skin, and straight stature. Each of these alone is found in other beings (e.g., a bird walks on two feet but has round nails; a bear has broad nails but is not bare-skinned; etc.), so they are not sufficient individually. However, when combined, they still do not uniquely identify humans? Actually the text says each of the four is found in other than human, so they are not exclusive. Then the fifth attribute 'laughing by nature' (ḍaḥḥāk bi al-ṭabʿ) is added, which is peculiar to humans, and then the set becomes a complete rasm. The commentator also addresses a potential objection: some of these attributes may be redundant (e.g., maybe bare skin implies straight stature), but this is not necessary to require non-redundancy; the purpose is only to give an example of how a complete rasm works. This aligns with the standard view that a rasm does not require essential attributes, only distinguishing accidental properties.

Complete vs. Incomplete Rasm: The Case of 'Laughing'
Author examines three interpretations of defining human as 'laughing' and determines the type of rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a definition by a single property is a complete or incomplete rasm by identifying the implied genus.

Core Idea
The same word yields different definition types depending on the implied genus.

When logicians define human as 'laughing', they must decide what genus is implied. The three possibilities—animal, body, or thing—lead to different classifications of rasm. This lesson unpacks those distinctions and shows how the author resolves a mismatch in the standard definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three interpretations
Find the three possible meanings of 'laughing' as a definition: the laughing animal (proximate genus), the thing that has laughter (broad genus), and the laughing body (distant genus).
2
Classify each as complete or incomplete rasm
According to the passage, 'laughing animal' is a complete rasm, while 'laughing body' is an incomplete rasm. The status of 'thing that has laughter' is ambiguous but often considered incomplete due to the excessive breadth of 'thing'.
The passage says the second interpretation is 'of that category', which likely refers to the first, but later commentators discuss its ambiguity.
3
Spot the mismatch with the standard definition
The matn defines incomplete rasm as 'composite of accidentals', but the case of distant genus + specific property (like 'laughing body') does not fall under accidentals. This reveals a gap.
4
Understand the solution: taghlib
Al-Fanari invokes taghlib (preponderance) to extend the term 'accidental' so that it covers specific properties when combined with a distant genus, thereby fitting the example into the category of incomplete rasm.
Taghlib is a rhetorical device where one term dominates another in a composite phrase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التعريف بالضاحك فقط، فإن أريد به الحيوان الضاحك فرسم تام. وإن أريد به الشيء الذي له الضحك فمن هذا القبيل. وأما إن أريد به الجسم الضاحك فقد ذكروا أنه أيضا أعني المركب من الجنس البعيد والخاصة رسم ناقص. مع أن ما ذكره ليس شاملا له فلا بد من التأويل إما بأن يقال: إنه من باب التغليب.
As for the definition by 'laughing' alone, if by it is meant 'the laughing animal', then it is a complete rasm. And if by it is meant 'the thing that has laughter', then it is of that category. And if by it is meant 'the laughing body', they mentioned that it also, i.e., the compound of distant genus and specific property, is an incomplete rasm. Even though what he mentioned is not inclusive of it, so there must be interpretation, either by saying: it is of the type of preponderance.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of human simply as 'laughing' (al-ḍāḥik) and evaluates three possible meanings. First, if 'laughing' is understood as 'the laughing animal' (al-ḥayawān al-ḍāḥik), then it is a complete rasm because 'animal' is the proximate genus and 'laughing' is the specific property. Second, if it means 'the thing that has laughter' (al-shay' alladhī lahu al-ḍiḥk), then it is also from this category (i.e., a complete rasm? Actually the text says 'famin hādhā al-qabīl' which could mean it falls under the same type, or it could be vague. The commentary likely means it is also a complete rasm because the genus 'thing' is too broad? Actually the text says 'famin hādhā al-qabīl' which might refer to the previous category of complete rasm? But then it says 'if by it is meant the laughing body, then it is an incomplete rasm.' So the second interpretation might be ambiguous. The commentator Al-Fanari clarifies that the standard logicians mention that if the genus is the distant genus 'body' (jism), then the definition is an incomplete rasm because the genus is too remote and the specific property is not sufficient to fully define. The passage then notes that the author's (of the matn) definition of incomplete rasm as 'composite of accidentals' does not include this case (compound of distant genus and specific property). Therefore, an interpretation is needed: that the author intended 'accidentals' to include both accidental properties and specific properties when they are combined with a distant genus, by way of preponderance (taghlīb). This is a typical solution in Islamic logic where the term 'accidental' ('araḍī) is sometimes used broadly to cover properties that are not essential in the definition.

تغليب
