Classifying Descriptive Definitions and Evaluating Their Utility
Author classifies definitions by 'al-dahik' and responds to an objection about utility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any four-term description as rasm tamm or rasm naqis and assess whether a definition that includes a general accident is beneficial.

Core Idea
A definition built on a general accident fails to distinguish or reveal essence, defeating its purpose.

This passage continues the discussion of definitions by examining the phrase 'al-dahik'. It shows how different interpretations of this phrase yield different types of descriptions (rasm). More importantly, it raises a critical objection: some descriptions that include a general accident like 'thing' provide no real benefit, because they neither distinguish the definiendum nor reveal its essence. Understanding this helps us evaluate whether a given definition actually serves its purpose in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus in the definition
Look at the term being used as the broader category. Is it a proximate genus like 'animal' or a remote genus like 'body'? Also check if it is a general accident like 'thing'.
In the passage, 'laughing animal' uses 'animal' (proximate genus), 'laughing body' uses 'body' (remote genus), and 'laughing thing' uses 'thing' (general accident).
2
Identify the property (khassa)
Find the term that indicates a proper accidental property that is coextensive with the definiendum but not essential. For humans, 'laughing' is such a property.
3
Classify as rasm tamm or rasm naqis
If the genus is proximate and the property is a proper accident, it is rasm tamm. If the genus is remote or if a general accident is used, it is rasm naqis.
The passage says 'laughing animal' is rasm tamm, 'laughing body' is rasm naqis, and 'laughing thing' is also problematic.
4
Evaluate the benefit of the definition
Check if the definition achieves distinction (tamyiz) of the definiendum from other things and/or reveals its essence (ittila' 'ala al-dhat). A definition that uses a general accident does neither, as the accident is too broad.
The objection in the passage states that 'laughing thing' is useless because 'thing' applies to everything and does not distinguish humans from other things.
5
Consider the resolution via ta'wil
If the definition seems not to fit the classification or appears useless, the author offers three interpretive ways (taghlib, applying whole to part, or considering common usage) to possibly salvage it. Reflect on whether these interpretations resolve the issue.
The passage leaves the matter open, implying that the objection may not be fully answered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملتزم، والغرض التمثيل. وأما التعريف بالضاحك فقط، فإن أريد به الحيوان الضاحك فرسم تام. وإن أريد به الشيء الذي له الضحك فمن هذا القبيل. وأما إن أريد به الجسم الضاحك فقد ذكروا أنه أيضا أعني المركب من الجنس البعيد والخاصة رسم ناقص. مع أن ما ذكره ليس شاملا له فلا بد من التأويل إما بأن يقال: إنه من باب التغليب. أو من إطلاق اسم الكل على الجزء، فإن مجموع المركب من الذاتي والعرضي عرضي. أو يقال: ذكر ما هو الغالب في الوقوع. فإن قلت: الشيء الضاحك مركب من العرض العام والخاصة فلا فائدة فيه; لأن العرض العام لا يفيد التمييز ولا الاطلاع على الذات. والتعريف لإحدى الفائدتين. ومثله التعريف بالفصل والخاصة. قلت: قد قيل ذلك إن حقا وإن كذبا.
Committed, and the purpose is illustration. As for the definition by 'the laughing one' alone: if what is meant is 'the laughing animal', then it is a complete description. And if what is meant is 'the thing that has laughter', then it belongs to this category. But if what is meant is 'the laughing body', then they mentioned that it also—that is, the composite of the remote genus and the property—is an incomplete description. However, what he mentioned does not cover it, so ta'wil (interpretation) is necessary, either by saying it is by way of predominance (taghlib), or by way of applying the name of the whole to the part, for the entire composite of the essential and the accidental is accidental, or by saying: what is mentioned is what occurs most often. If you say: the laughing thing is composed of the general accident and the property, so there is no benefit in it, because the general accident does not yield distinction nor attainment of the essence, and definition is for one of these two benefits. And similar to it is the definition by the differentia and the property. I say: that has been said, whether truly or falsely.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of definitions, specifically the types of descriptive definitions (rasm). The author begins by stating the purpose of providing examples, then examines the definition 'laughing one' (al-dahik). If 'laughing one' is interpreted as 'laughing animal', that is a complete description (rasm tamm) because it combines a proximate genus (animal) with a property (laughing). If it is interpreted as 'thing that has laughter', it falls under another category (perhaps a loose definition). If it is interpreted as 'laughing body', the author notes that logicians consider this a composite of remote genus (body) and property (laughing), which is an incomplete description (rasm naqis). However, the author of the text (the matn) did not explicitly mention this composite, so interpretation is needed to fit it into his classification. Three interpretations are offered: (1) by taghlib (predominance), where the accidental dominates the essential; (2) by applying the name of the whole to the part, since the composite of essential and accidental is considered accidental; (3) by saying the expression refers to what is most common. Then an objection is raised: 'The laughing thing' is composed of a general accident ('thing') and a property (laughing), but such a definition yields no benefit because the general accident does not distinguish the thing nor reveal its essence, and definition aims at one of these two benefits. The same objection applies to defining by differentia and property together. The response is that this objection has been made, whether it is true or false—meaning it is a known objection, but not necessarily accepted. The author leaves the matter without fully settling it, implying that the objection may be answered in other ways.

Key Takeaway
A definition that uses a general accident fails to achieve the purpose of definition, whether it is for distinction or for revealing the essence.

Classification and Utility of Definitions with Accidental Attributes
Defense of definitions containing accidental attributes and classification into hadd and rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a definition as hadd (complete or incomplete) or rasm, and justify the benefit of definitions that include accidental attributes.

Core Idea
Definitions with non-essential attributes are not useless; they enhance conceptualization beyond mere essential or property alone.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection that definitions combining a general accident (like 'existent') with a special property (like 'laughing') are pointless because the general accident does not distinguish or reveal essence. The author defends their utility, explaining that such definitions provide a stronger conceptualization than using only the special property or only the differentia. The passage then gives the final classification of definitions: those composed solely of essential attributes are called hadd (the has decided to use: complete if all essentials, incomplete if only some), while definitions using non-essentials, such as proximate genus and special property, are called rasm (complete description).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its grounds
The objector claims that defining 'laughing thing' (general accident + special property) is useless because the general accident does not discriminate or give insight into the essence. Definition is supposed to provide one of these benefits.
The objection is based on a strict view that only essential attributes are informative.
2
Trace the author's counter-argument
The author asserts that even if true, those definitions are beneficial because conceptualization with both general accident and special property is stronger than with special property alone. Similarly, adding special property to differentia enhances conceptualization.
The key phrase is 'أقوى' (stronger) – the benefit is incremental.
3
Extract the final classification of definitions
The author states: definition by essential attributes alone (all = hadd tamm, some = hadd naqis). Definition not by essential attributes alone (e.g., proximate genus + special property) is rasm tamm (complete description).
Note that 'rasm' here is specifically the complete type using proximate genus and special property.
4
Apply the classification to an Islamic example
Consider defining 'ḥadd al-sariqa' (theft punishment). A hadd tamm would use genus (penalty) and differentia (specific type). A rasm might use proximate genus (punishment) and special property (cutting the hand for theft). The rasm is still useful for identification.
This shows how practical definitions in fiqh often use non-essential attributes.

أو مِنْ إطلاق اسمِ الكلِّ على الجُزء، فإنَّ مجموعَ المُرَكَّبِ مِنَ الذاتي والعرضي عَرَضي. أو يُقالُ: ذكر ما هوَ الغالِبُ في الوقوع. فإنْ قُلْتَ: الشَّيءُ الضاحكُ مُرَكَّبٌ من العَرَضِ العام والخاصة فلا فائدة فيه؛ لأنَّ العَرَضَ العام لا يُفيدُ التَّمْيِيزَ ولا الاطلاع على الذات. والتّعريفُ لإحدى الفائدتين. ومثله التّعريفُ بالفَصل والخاصة. قلتُ : قد قِيْلَ ذلك إنْ حَقا وإنْ كَذِبًا. أما الحقُّ الحَقيقُ بالقَبُولِ - فإنَّ التصوُّرَ مَعَ العَرَضِ العَامّ والخاصَّة أقوى من التَصَوِّر مَعَ مجرّدِ الخاصة، وكذا التَصَوُّرُ مَعَ الفَصْلِ والخاصَّة أقوى من التصور مع مجرد الفصل؛ فكيف لا يكون لهما - أي للعرض العام والخاصة - فائدة. فالضبط: أَنَّ التَّعريف بمجرد الذاتيات بمجموعها - حد تام وببعضها حد ناقص. والتَّعريفُ لا بمُجَرِّدِ الذاتياتِ، فبالجنس القريبِ والخاصَّةِ رَسُمٌ تام.
Or it is from calling the whole by the part, for the sum of what is composed of the essential and accidental is accidental. Or it is said: mentioning what is prevalent in occurrence. If you say: The laughing thing is composed of general accident and special property, so there is no benefit in it, because the general accident does not give distinction nor insight into the essence. And definition is for one of the two benefits. And likewise definition by the differentia and special property. I say: That has been said, whether truly or falsely. As for the truth worthy of acceptance: For conceptualization with the general accident and special property is stronger than conceptualization with mere special property, and likewise conceptualization with the differentia and special property is stronger than conceptualization with mere differentia; so how can there not be benefit for them? The rule: Definition by mere essential attributes in their entirety is a complete definition, and by some of them an incomplete definition. And definition not by mere essential attributes, then by the proximate genus and special property is a complete description.

Commentary
This passage addresses a common objection against definitions that include accidental attributes, and then presents the final classification of definitions. The author begins by noting that a composite of essential and accidental is considered accidental, and that sometimes definitions mention what is predominant. Then the objection is raised: defining 'man' as 'laughing' (a combination of general accident 'thing' and special property 'laughing') is useless because the general accident does not distinguish the thing nor reveal its essence; and definition is supposed to provide one of these two benefits. The same is said for definitions combining differentia and special property. The author responds that this claim has been made, but the correct view is that such definitions are beneficial because they provide a stronger conceptualization than using only the special property or only the differentia alone. Hence, they do serve a purpose. Finally, the rule is given: a definition composed solely of essential attributes (all or some) is called hadd (complete or incomplete respectively). A definition not composed solely of essential attributes, such as one with the proximate genus and special property, is called rasm (description).

Classification of Definitions: Complete and Incomplete Hadd and Rasm
Author classifies definitions into complete/incomplete hadd and rasm with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any definition as complete or incomplete hadd or rasm based on its components.

Core Idea
Definitions are either hadd (essential) or rasm (accidental); completeness depends on inclusion of all essentials or proximate genus+proper accident.

In this passage, the author establishes a clear rule for judging definitions. You will learn the two families of definition and their completeness conditions, enabling you to evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
List the terms used in the definition: genus (جنس), differentia (فصل), property (خاصة), common accident (عرض عام). For example, 'human is a laughing animal' includes genus (animal) and property (laughing).
Standard definitions use a combination of these.
2
Check for essential properties only
If the definition uses only genus and/or differentia (or parts of them) and no accidents, it is a hadd. If it includes any accidental property (خاصة or عرض عام), it is a rasm.
Essential properties (ذاتيات) are those without which the thing cannot be conceived.
3
Evaluate completeness for hadd
If the definition includes all essential properties (genus and differentia), it is a complete hadd (حد تام). If it includes only some (e.g., only differentia), it is an incomplete hadd (حد ناقص).
Example: 'human is rational animal' is complete hadd; 'human is rational' is incomplete hadd.
4
Evaluate completeness for rasm
If the rasm uses the proximate genus (الجنس القريب) and the proper accident (الخاصة), it is a complete rasm (رسم تام). Otherwise (e.g., remote genus, or only one component), it is an incomplete rasm (رسم ناقص).
Example: 'human is laughing animal' is complete rasm; 'human is laughing body' is incomplete rasm.
5
Classify and verify with passage examples
Apply the rules to the six examples given: e.g., 'human is a thing that is rational' (common accident + differentia) → incomplete rasm; 'human is rational, laughing' (differentia + property) → incomplete rasm.
The passage confirms that all examples lacking proximate genus and proper accident together are incomplete rasm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحقُّ الحقيقُ بالقَبُولِ - فإنَّ التصوُّرَ مَعَ العَرَضِ العَامّ والخاصَّة أقوى من التَصَوِّر مَعَ مجرّدِ الخاصة، وكذا التَصَوُّرُ مَعَ الفَصْلِ والخاصَّة أقوى من التصور مع مجرد الفصل؛ فكيف لا يكون لهما - أي للعرض العام والخاصة - فائدة.
أي: الغرض من التعريف أحد الفائدتين أعلاه لا نسلم فيه؛ لأن التعريف ليس محصوراً فيهما، بل قد يفيد أمراً آخر، فإنه قد يؤتى بالتعريف لأمور أخرى، كأن يكون أكمل، فإنَّ التعريف بالعَرَضِ العام مع الخاصة، مثل: الإنسان ضاحك متنفس أكمل من التعريف بالخاصة التي جعلتموها صالحة للتعريف وحدها، وأيضا المركب من الفصل والخاصة مثل الإنسان ناطق ضاحك، والمركب من العرض العام والفصل أكمل من التعريف بالفصل وحده مثل الإنسان ضاحك.
فالضبط: أنَّ التَّعريف بمجرد الذاتيات بمجموعها - حد تام وببعضها حد ناقص. والتَّعريفُ لا بمُجَرَّدِ الذاتيات، فبالجنس القريبِ والخاصَّةِ رَسُم تام. وبغيرِهِ رَسْمُ ناقص. فعلى هذا العَرَضُ العَامُّ مَعَ الفَصْل، أو الخاصة، والخاصة مع الفصل، والجنسُ البَعيدُ مع الخاصةِ كُلَّ مِنْها رَسُمٌ ناقِصُ. وذلك فيما يأتي: التعريف بالعرض العام مع الفصل مثل: الإنسان شيء ناطق. والتعريف بالعرض العام مع الخاصة مثل: الإنسان شيء ضاحك. والتعريف بالخاصة مع الفصل مثل: الإنسان ناطق ضاحك. والتعريف بالجنس البعيد مع الخاصة مثل: الإنسان جسم ضاحك. والتعريف بالخاصة وحدها مثل: الإنسان ضاحك. والتعريف بالفصل وحده مثل: الإنسان ناطق. كلها رسم ناقص.
As for the truth worthy of acceptance: conception with common accident and property is stronger than conception with mere property; likewise, conception with differentia and property is stronger than conception with mere differentia. So how could they (i.e., common accident and property) not have benefit? That is: we do not concede that the purpose of definition is one of the two benefits mentioned above, because definition is not confined to them; rather it may benefit something else, as a definition may be brought for other purposes, such as being more complete. Indeed, definition by common accident with property, like 'man is laughing, breathing', is more complete than definition by the property which you consider sufficient for definition alone; also the compound of differentia and property, like 'man is rational, laughing'; and the compound of common accident and differentia is more complete than definition by differentia alone, like 'man is laughing'. So the rule: by mere essential properties alone, if with all of them it is a complete definition; if with some of them, an incomplete definition. Definition not by mere essential properties: with proximate genus and property it is a complete descriptive definition; with other than that, an incomplete descriptive definition. Accordingly, common accident with differentia, or with property, property with differentia, and remote genus with property, each of them is an incomplete descriptive definition. That is as follows: definition by common accident with differentia, like 'man is a thing that is rational'; definition by common accident with property, like 'man is a thing that laughs'; definition by property with differentia, like 'man is rational, laughing'; definition by remote genus with property, like 'man is a body that laughs'; definition by property alone, like 'man is laughing'; definition by differentia alone, like 'man is rational'. All of these are incomplete descriptive definitions.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a classification of definitions into complete and incomplete types, both for definitions by essential properties (hadd) and descriptive definitions (rasm). The author begins by defending the utility of common accident and property in definitions, arguing that a definition combining these with more essential components yields a stronger conception. Then he presents the core rule: a definition composed solely of essential properties (dhatiyyat) is a hadd; if it includes all of them, it is a complete hadd; if only some, an incomplete hadd. Definitions that include non-essential properties (such as common accident or proper accident) are termed rasm. When the definition uses the proximate genus along with the proper accident, it is a complete rasm; otherwise (e.g., if it uses a more distant genus, omits the genus, or uses only a single accident) it is an incomplete rasm. The author then enumerates six specific cases of incomplete rasm, each illustrated with the example of 'man'. These examples are drawn from the Islamic logical tradition: for instance, 'man is a rational laughing animal' combines differentia and property, while 'man is a laughing body' uses remote genus and property. The classification shows that the completeness of a definition depends on the type and combination of its components, and that even incomplete definitions can be useful.

Recognizing Incomplete Definitions: The Six Types of Rasm Naqis
Author enumerates six types of incomplete definitions and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any definition as complete or incomplete (rasm naqis) by identifying whether the proximate genus is present, and name the specific type of deficiency when it is absent.

Core Idea
The proximate genus is the key to a complete definition; without it, every combination of universals falls short.

In the previous lesson, you learned that a complete definition (hadd) requires the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarīb) along with the differentia. But what happens when the proximate genus is missing? The definition becomes a deficient description (rasm nāqiṣ). This passage from al-Fanārī enumerates six specific types of deficient definitions, each illustrated with the example of 'human' (insān). By mastering these types, you will be able to quickly spot incomplete definitions in any Islamic science text.

Guided Path
1
Recall the components of a complete definition
A complete definition (hadd haqiqi) consists of the proximate genus and the differentia. For 'human', the proximate genus is 'animal' and the differentia is 'rational'. Any definition lacking the proximate genus is deficient.
The proximate genus is the immediate genus; the remote genus (e.g., 'body') is too broad.
2
Identify the six deficient combinations
The author lists six combinations that produce a rasm naqis: (1) common accident + differentia, (2) common accident + proprium, (3) proprium + differentia, (4) remote genus + proprium, (5) proprium alone, (6) differentia alone. Read each example in the Arabic text.
All these lack the proximate genus. The differentia alone or proprium alone are even more deficient.
3
Analyze each example using the 'human' model
For each type, check which universals are present. For instance, 'Human is a speaking thing' uses 'thing' (common accident) and 'speaking' (differentia) – no genus at all, let alone proximate. 'Human is a laughing body' uses 'body' (remote genus) and 'laughing' (proprium) – the genus is remote, not proximate.
The remote genus 'body' includes non-humans, so it fails to specify.
4
Generalize the rule
Any definition that does not contain the proximate genus is a rasm naqis, regardless of what other universals it includes. The six types are exhaustive of the possible combinations that exclude the proximate genus.
The key is to always check for the presence of the immediate genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And with other than it is a deficient definition. So on this basis, the common accident with the differentia, or the proprium, and the proprium with the differentia, and the remote genus with the proprium—each of these is a deficient definition. (Meaning: other than the proximate genus.) (Meaning: the absence of the proximate genus in the definition makes the definition deficient.) And that occurs in the following: Definition by common accident and differentia, like 'Human is a speaking thing.' Definition by common accident and proprium, like 'Human is a laughing thing.' Definition by proprium and differentia, like 'Human is a laughing, speaking.' Definition by remote genus and proprium, like 'Human is a laughing body.' Definition by proprium alone, like 'Human is laughing.' Definition by differentia alone, like 'Human is speaking.' All are deficient definitions.

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'deficient definition' (rasm naqis) and enumerates its six types. The key condition for a complete definition is the inclusion of the proximate genus. When the proximate genus is absent, the definition is deficient. The author lists the possible combinations that yield a deficient definition: common accident with differentia, common accident with proprium, proprium with differentia, remote genus with proprium, proprium alone, and differentia alone. Each is illustrated with the example of 'human' (insān). For instance, 'Human is a speaking thing' uses the differentia 'speaking' without the genus, making it deficient. Similarly, 'Human is a laughing body' uses the remote genus 'body' instead of the proximate genus. These examples help the student recognize which definitions lack the necessary component.

The Eight Conditions for Genuine Contradiction
Author enumerates eight conditions for contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight conditions for contradiction and identify which condition is violated in given proposition pairs.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires agreement in eight aspects; otherwise both can be true.

In this passage, the author enumerates eight units (subject, predicate, time, place, relation, potentiality/actuality, part/whole, condition) that two propositions must share to be true contradictories. We will examine each condition with its example to understand how differences destroy contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Examine the list of conditions
Read the passage and note the eight conditions: subject, predicate, time, place, relation, potentiality/actuality, part/whole, condition. The author uses examples like Zayd and Amr for subject, etc.
The conditions are also called 'units'.
2
Understand the consequence of violating any condition
For each condition, a counterexample shows that if the condition is not met, the two propositions could both be true (or both false), so they are not contradictories. For example, if subjects differ, both 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing' can be true simultaneously.
3
Practice identifying violations
Given a pair like 'Zayd is standing in the mosque' and 'Zayd is not standing in the market', identify the violated condition (place) and explain why they are not contradictory.

١. ( في المَوْضُوع) بخِلافِ زيدٌ قائم وعمرو ليسَ بقائم.
٢. (والمَحْمُولِ) بخلافِ زيد قائم وزيدٌ ليس بقاعدٍ. (والزمان) بخلافِ زيد قائم أي في الليل وزَيْدٌ ليس بقائم أي في النهار.
(والمَكانِ) بخلافِ زيدٌ قائم - أي في المسْجِدِ وزيد ليس بقائم – أي في السوق.

وإلا فلا حَصْرُ فيما ذكروه؛
لارتفاع التناقض باختلافِ الآلة نحو زيد كاتب أي بالقلمِ الواسطي زيد ليس بكاتب أي بالقَلَمِ التُركي.
(۱) شروط التناقض أن تتحد الجملتان في الأمور التي ذكرها الشارح وتسمى (وحدة النسبة الحكمية؛ لأنه إذا لم تتفق فلربما تصدقان أو تكذبان والتناقض يوجب أن تكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة. (۲) فإذا كان خالد له ابن اسمه محمد، فخالد أب، بالإضافة لمحمد ومحمد ابن بالإضافة إلى خالد. (۳) أي : فيه قوة الإسكار، ويكون بالفعل عند شربه. (٤) هو الحبشي؛ لأنه من سكان القارة الإفريقية السوداء. (٥) لأن في حدقات عينيه بياضاً، وكذا أسنانه. (٦) كالورق والقماش الأبيض الناصع. (٧) مفرق أي مضعف مؤثر على قوة نظره. (٨) لأنه إذا تغير شيء في النقيض قد لا يتحقق صدق إحداهما وكذب الأخرى بذاتها وأنت ترى أن ما يخالف الشرط تكون كلا القضيتين صادقتين.
Conditions for the validity of contradiction. (And that does not occur) i.e., the described difference in the two particular propositions except after their agreement (i.e., the two propositions) in eight units:
1. In the subject, unlike Zayd is standing and Amr is not standing.
2. And in the predicate, unlike Zayd is standing and Zayd is not sitting. And in time, unlike Zayd is standing at night and Zayd is not standing in the daytime.
And in place, unlike Zayd is standing in the mosque and Zayd is not standing in the market.
5. And in relation, unlike Zayd is a father of Amr, Zayd is not a father of Bakr.
6. And in potentiality and actuality, unlike wine in the jar is intoxicating potentially, and wine in the jar is not intoxicating actually.
And in part and whole, unlike the black person is black i.e., some of him is not black i.e., all of him.
And in condition, unlike the body is harmful to sight with the condition of its whiteness and not harmful to sight with the condition of its blackness.
And the correct view is that what is considered in achieving contradiction is unity of the relative judgment, so that affirmation and negation bear upon one thing.
For its unity entails these unities.
And the lack of unity of any of them entails lack of unity of the relative judgment,
otherwise there is no restriction to what they mentioned;
because contradiction is removed by difference in instrument, like Zayd is a writer with the Wasiti pen, Zayd is not a writer with the Turkish pen.
(1) The conditions of contradiction are that the two statements agree in the matters mentioned by the commentator, called 'unity of relative judgment'; because if they do not agree, maybe both are true or both false, while contradiction requires one to be true and the other false. (2) If Khalid has a son named Muhammad, then Khalid is a father in relation to Muhammad, and Muhammad is a son in relation to Khalid. (3) i.e., in it is the potential for intoxication, and it becomes actual upon drinking. (4) i.e., the Abyssinian, because he is from the inhabitants of the black continent. (5) Because in the pupils of his eyes there is whiteness, and likewise his teeth. (6) Like paper and cloth that are pure white. (7) 'Mufarriq' means weakening, affecting his vision. (8) Because if something changes in the contradictory, perhaps the truth of one and the falsehood of the other is not realized by itself, and you see that what violates the condition makes both propositions true.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for two propositions to be contradictory (tanāquḍ). The author begins by stating that the described difference (i.e., one true, one false) between two particular propositions only occurs after the propositions agree in eight aspects (units). These are listed with examples: 1) Subject: if subjects differ, no contradiction (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' vs 'Amr is not standing'). 2) Predicate: if predicates differ, no contradiction (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' vs 'Zayd is not sitting'). 3) Time: e.g., 'standing at night' vs 'not standing in daytime'. 4) Place: e.g., 'standing in mosque' vs 'not standing in market'. 5) Relation: e.g., 'father of Amr' vs 'not father of Bakr'. 6) Potentiality vs actuality: e.g., wine is intoxicating potentially but not actually. 7) Part vs whole: e.g., a black person is black in part (some parts like teeth are white) but not black as a whole. 8) Condition: e.g., a bright body harms sight when white but not when black. After listing, the author gives the correct criterion: the real condition is unity of the relative judgment (waḥdat al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya), meaning affirmation and negation must apply to exactly the same thing. This unity entails the eight conditions, but the absence of any one condition does not necessarily negate the possibility of contradiction—there are other factors like instrument (e.g., writing with different pens). The passage thus corrects a potential misunderstanding that the eight conditions are exhaustive; rather, the essential condition is unity of the relative judgment. The footnotes provide elaboration: e.g., note (1) explains that when conditions are not met, both propositions could be true or false; note (2) gives an example of relation; notes (3)-(8) clarify the examples.

Key Terms
تناقض (tanāquḍ): Contradiction between two propositions where one is true and the other false, requiring agreement in subject, predicate, time, place, etc., but ultimately defined by unity of relative judgment. [Note: In earlier logic texts, tanāquḍ often treated as simple opposition without the detailed conditions; here it is refined with a focus on relative judgment.]
وحدة النسبة الحكمية (waḥdat al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya): The unity of the relative judgment, meaning the affirmation and negation apply to exactly the same thing in every respect, which is the true condition for contradiction. [Note: This term is highlighted by later commentators as the core condition, whereas earlier works might list conditions without explicitly stating this unity.]
قوة وفعل (quwwa wa fiʿl): Potentiality and actuality; a distinction used to avoid false contradiction when something has a property potentially but not actually. [Note: Stable term across logic and philosophy; no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs is NOT a genuine contradiction because it violates the condition of 'relation'?
A) 'Zayd is a father of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a father of Bakr'.
B) 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating' and 'Wine in the jar is not intoxicating' (potential/actual).
C) 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' (same time and place).
D) 'The black person is black' and 'The black person is not black' (part/whole).

Key Takeaway
The eight conditions—subject, predicate, time, place, relation, potentiality/actuality, part/whole, condition—must be satisfied for two propositions to be genuine contradictories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the condition violated in the pair: 'Zayd is a father of Khalid' and 'Zayd is not a father of Khalid's brother'. Explain why they are not contradictory.
?
Given the pair: 'The body is white under sunlight' and 'The body is not white under candlelight', which condition is missing? (Answer: condition/respect)

The Essence of Contradiction: Unity of Relative Judgment
Author states the correct view is unity of relative judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why unity of relative judgment is the fundamental condition for contradiction and how it subsumes the eight conditions.

Core Idea
Unity of relative judgment is the master condition that makes the eight conditions redundant.

After listing eight conditions, the author declares the correct view: the real condition for contradiction is that affirmation and negation apply to exactly the same thing. This is called 'unity of relative judgment.' We will see why it is the essential condition and how it encapsulates the eight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core statement
Locate the sentence: 'والصحيح : أن المعتبر في تحقق التناقض وحدة النسبة الحكمية حتى يرد الإيجاب والسلب على شيء واحد'. This is the author's refinement of the eight conditions.
This sentence shifts from enumerative to synthetic criterion.
2
Understand the relationship between unity and the eight conditions
The author says unity of relative judgment entails the eight conditions, but the eight are not exhaustive. Unity is the single condition that makes all differences of aspect irrelevant. For instance, difference in instrument (Wasiti pen vs Turkish pen) is not covered by the eight, but unity of relative judgment catches it.
The footnote gives the instrument example.
3
Apply unity to detect false contradictions
Consider the example: 'Zayd is a writer with a Wasiti pen' and 'Zayd is not a writer with a Turkish pen'. These are not contradictory because the relative judgment (the 'thing' affirmed/negated) differs: writing with one pen vs another. Unity is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشروط صحة التناقض (ولا يتحقق ذلك) أي الاخْتِلافُ الموصوف في المخصوصتين: إلا بعد اتفاقِهُما ) أي القَضِيَتَيْنِ فِي ثَمانية وحدات):
١. ( في المَوْضُوع) بخِلافِ زيدٌ قائم وعمرو ليسَ بقائم.
٢. (والمَحْمُولِ) بخلافِ زيد قائم وزيدٌ ليس بقاعدٍ. (والزمان) بخلافِ زيد قائم أي في الليل وزَيْدٌ ليس بقائم أي في النهار.
(والمَكانِ) بخلافِ زيدٌ قائم - أي في المسْجِدِ وزيد ليس بقائم – أي في السوق.
.٥ (والإضافة) بخلافِ زيدٌ أبٌ أي لعمرو، زيد ليس بأب أي لبكر.
٦ (والقوة والفعل) بخلافِ الخمرُ في الدنِ مُسْكِرٌ - أي بالقوة) والخمر في الدن ليس بمُسْكر - أي بالفعل.
(والجزء والكُلّ) بخلافِ الزنجي أسودُ أي: بعضُه ليس بأسودَ أي كله.
(والشرط) بخلافِ الجِسمُ مُفَرِّقُ للبصَرِ أَي بشَرطِ بياضِهِ وغيرُ مفرّق للبصر - أي بشرطِ سَوادِهِ.

فإِنَّ وحدتها مستلزمة لهذه الوحدات.
وعدم وحدة الشيء منها مستلزمةٌ لعدمِ وَحدَةِ النسبةِ الحُكميَّةِ،
وإلا فلا حَصْرُ فيما ذكروه؛
لارتفاع التناقض باختلافِ الآلة نحو زيد كاتب أي بالقلمِ الواسطي زيد ليس بكاتب أي بالقَلَمِ التُركي.

(۲) فإذا كان خالد له ابن اسمه محمد، فخالد أب، بالإضافة لمحمد ومحمد ابن بالإضافة إلى خالد. (۳) أي : فيه قوة الإسكار، ويكون بالفعل عند شربه. (٤) هو الحبشي؛ لأنه من سكان القارة الإفريقية السوداء. (٥) لأن في حدقات عينيه بياضاً، وكذا أسنانه. (٦) كالورق والقماش الأبيض الناصع. (٧) مفرق أي مضعف مؤثر على قوة نظره. Definition of Demonstration and the Five Arts
Defining demonstration and situating it among the five arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define demonstration (burhan) by its genus and differentia, and distinguish it from the other four arts of logic.

Core Idea
Demonstration is the syllogism of certainty; it is one of five arts defined by their premises.

Having completed the study of syllogistic form, the logician must now examine the matter—the types of premises. This chapter introduces the five arts of logic, each serving a distinct purpose. At the pinnacle is burhan (demonstration), which yields certain knowledge (yaqin). In this lesson, we will dissect its definition and understand how it relates to the other arts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The definiendum is 'burhan' (demonstration). Locate it in the Arabic passage.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'qiyas' (syllogism). This places demonstration in the larger category of syllogistic reasoning.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is 'mu'allaf min muqaddimat yaqiniyya li-intaj al-yaqin' — composed of certain premises to produce certainty. This distinguishes it from other arts like dialectic (which uses probable premises) and rhetoric (which uses persuasive premises).
The passage specifies that premises can be necessary (daruriyya) or acquired (muktasaba).
4
Test the definition's completeness
Check whether the definition includes all instances and excludes others. It includes all demonstrative syllogisms, whether the certainty arises from necessity or acquisition. It excludes rhetoric, dialectic, sophistry, and poetics.
5
Situate among the five arts
Recognize that burhan is the first of the five arts. The other four are: jadal (dialectic), khitaba (rhetoric), shi'r (poetics), and safsata (sophistry). Each has a distinct type of premises and goal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ أبوابِ المَنْطِقِ أبواب الصناعات الخمْسِ؛ لأنَّ المنطِقِيَّ كما يَبحَثُ عَنْ الصُّوْرَةِ يَبحَثُ عن المادَّةِ. فلمَّا تَمَّ التلويحُ إلى مباحثِ الصوْرَةِ أَشَارَ إِلَى مَباحثِ المَادَّةِ أَيضاً فقالَ: مِنْ جملة الصناعاتِ الخَمْسِ: البرهانُ وهو قياس مؤلَّفٌ مِنْ مقدِّماتٍ يقينية لإنتاج اليَقِينِ أَعمُّ مِنْ أن تَكُونَ ضرورِيَّةٌ أو مكْتَسَبةٌ منها. فالقِياسُ : جِنسٌ يتناوَلُ الأقيسةَ الخَمْسَ. والمؤلَّفُ: ذُكِرَ ليتعلقَ بهِ قولُهُ مِنْ مقدَّماتٍ يَقِينيَةٍ وَهوَ: يُخْرِجُ الخطابة، والجدل، وغيرهما. صناعة، وهي ملكة نفسانية تصدر عنها الأفعال الاختيارية من غير رَوِيَّةٍ، وقيل : العلم المتعلق بكيفية العمل.
And among the chapters of logic are the chapters of the five arts; because the logician, just as he investigates form, investigates matter. When the indication of the investigations of form was completed, he also pointed to the investigations of matter, saying: From the number of the five arts: Demonstration, which is a syllogism composed of certain premises for producing certainty, general meaning that they are either necessary or acquired from them. For 'syllogism' is a genus that includes the five syllogisms. And 'composed' is mentioned so that his statement 'of certain premises' may attach to it, and it excludes rhetoric, dialectic, and others. 'Craft' (sina'ah) is a psychic disposition from which voluntary actions issue without deliberation, and it is said: knowledge pertaining to the manner of action.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the five arts (al-sana'i' al-khams). The author explains that the logician studies both the form (surah) of syllogisms and their matter (maddah). Having completed the discussion of form, he now turns to matter. He defines demonstration (burhan) as a syllogism composed of certain premises (muqaddimat yaqiniyya) to produce certainty (yaqin). The premises can be either necessary (daruriyya) or acquired (muktasaba) from them. The term 'syllogism' is the genus for the five types of syllogism (demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetics, sophistry). The word 'composed' is inserted so that 'of certain premises' can modify it, and this excludes non-demonstrative syllogisms like rhetoric and dialectic. The author also defines 'craft' (sina'ah) as a psychic disposition (malaka nafsaniyya) from which voluntary actions proceed without deliberation, or alternatively as knowledge of how to perform an action.

Key Terms
البرهان (al-burhan): A syllogism composed of certain premises to produce certain knowledge (yaqin). [Note: Stable; in Aristotelian logic, demonstration is the highest form of reasoning.]
الصناعة (al-sina'ah): A psychic faculty for performing actions without deliberation; here referring to the five arts of logic. [Note: In broader usage, sina'ah means craft or skill; in logic it is a technical term for the type of syllogism.]
قياس (qiyas): Syllogism as a genus encompassing the five types of reasoning in the five arts. [Note: In fiqh, qiyas means analogical deduction; here it is categorical syllogism.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of the premises in a demonstrative syllogism (burhan)?
A) They are probable
B) They are certain (yaqiniyya)
C) They are persuasive
D) They are poetic

Key Takeaway
Demonstration is a syllogism composed of certain premises to produce certainty; it is the highest of the five arts of logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
